Flowing Impurity from Other Body Parts: Nullification of Wudu
Ruling with commentary on nullification by flowing impurity from other body parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which flowing impurity nullifies wudu in the Hanafi school and distinguish it from solid impurity.

Core Idea
Flowing impurity nullifies wudu only if it flows to a place requiring purification; solid impurity never nullifies.

In this lesson, we examine a key nullifier of wudu: the emission of flowing impurity from any body part other than the two passages. The Hanafi school sets a specific condition—the impurity must actually flow to an area that requires purification. A simple drop that remains on the wound does not break wudu. This ruling highlights the school's precise method of defining nullification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nullifier
Note that the passage states that flowing impurity (najāsah sā'ilah) from other than the two passages nullifies wudu. Examples: blood and pus.
2
Understand the condition of flowing
The commentary defines 'sailan' as the impurity exceeding its source to a place that requires purification (even if recommended). If it stays within the same contiguous area (e.g., inside the eye), it does not nullify.
The eye example: blood flowing from one part of the eye to another remains in a single purification area, so no nullification.
3
Distinguish solid impurity
The passage ends with 'unlike solid'—solid impurities (like clotted blood) do not flow, hence do not nullify wudu. This is a key Hanafi distinction.
Solid impurity is excluded because it lacks the flowing property.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a cut on the hand: if blood oozes but stays on the wound, no nullification. If it runs down the finger, nullification occurs because it moves to a site requiring purification.
The test is whether the impurity moves to a part that is typically washed in wudu (e.g., arm, hand).
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that any impurity emitted from the body nullifies wudu regardless of flow. Hanafis require flowing.
This ikhtilaf affects practical rulings in daily worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونجاسة سائلة من ذكر وفرج غيرهما كدم وقيح. سائلة: اعلم أن السيلان في غير السبيلين بتجاوز النجاسة إلى محل يطلب تطهيره ولو ندباً، فلا ينقض دم سال في داخل العين إلى جانب آخر منها، بخلاف ما صلب من ا [مراقي الفلاح: ۸۷]
And flowing impurity from other than these two, like blood and pus. Flowing impurity: Know that flowing in other than the two passages is [defined as] the impurity exceeding to a place that requires purification, even if recommended. So blood flowing inside the eye to another side does not nullify, unlike solid [blood].
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the nullifier of flowing impurity (najisah sa'ilah) from any body part other than the two passages (front and rear). The main text mentions examples: blood and pus. The commentary clarifies that 'flowing' is defined as the impurity moving from its source to a place that requires purification (even if recommended, like the visible part of the body not normally covered?). If blood flows inside the eye (like from one corner to another), it does not nullify wudu because the entire interior of the eye is one contiguous place not requiring separate purification. However, if the impurity is solid (like clotted blood), it also does not nullify because it does not flow. The phrase 'bikhilafi ma salaba' refers to solid impurities that do not flow, and they do not nullify wudu.

Key Terms
نجاسة سائلة (najāsah sā'ilah): A liquid or flowing impurity; here specifically blood, pus, etc., that flows from other than the two passages.
سيلان (sayalān): Flowing; the movement of impurity from its source to another location that requires purification.
ما صلب (mā ṣalaba): What is solid; impurities that are solidified and do not flow, like clotted blood. These do not nullify wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what condition must be met for flowing impurity from other than the two passages to nullify wudu?
A) The impurity must be blood or pus.
B) The impurity must flow to a place that requires purification.
C) The impurity must be solid.
D) The impurity must come from a wound.

Key Takeaway
Flowing impurity nullifies wudu only when it flows from its source to a site that requires purification; solid impurities or non-flowing liquids do not nullify.

Nullifier: Vomit Filling the Mouth
Ruling and commentary on vomit nullifying wudu with condition of mouthful

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vomit nullifying wudu, define mil' al-fam (mouthful), and apply the rule on unity of cause for multiple vomits.

Core Idea
Vomit nullifies wudu only if it fills the mouth; multiple vomits from one nausea are counted together.

Vomiting is one of the nullifiers of wudu, but the Hanafi school imposes a specific condition: the vomit must fill the mouth. This lesson unpacks what constitutes a 'mouthful' and how to handle multiple vomits. You will learn the relied-upon position and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is vomiting (qay'), which can nullify wudu if it meets the condition. The ruling is that it is a nullifier (naqid), but only when the vomit fills the mouth.
2
Define mil' al-fam
Mil' al-fam means a mouthful: an amount that, when the mouth is closed, the lips do not naturally meet without effort. The most correct interpretation is that it prevents normal speech.
Some scholars define it as what prevents speech, others as what would fill the mouth if collected.
3
Apply the condition to types of vomit
The ruling applies to vomit of food, water, blood clot ('alaq), or bile (mirrah). It does not matter if the vomit comes immediately or later. The key is the amount.
4
Decide on multiple vomits: unity of cause vs. unity of sitting
If a person vomits several times, we must determine if they count as one event. Imam Muhammad considers the cause (nausea): if the second vomit occurs before the nausea settles (i.e., before the breath calms), it is united. Abu Yusuf considers the sitting: all vomits in one sitting count as one. The relied-upon view is Muhammad's.
This affects whether small amounts add up to a mouthful.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi ruling
The operative position: multiple vomits from the same nausea episode (without settling of breath) are combined. If the total fills the mouth, wudu is nullified. If the nausea settles between vomits, they are separate and each is judged individually.
This is the view preferred by Maraqi al-Falah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيء طعام أو ماء أو علق أو مرة إذا ملأ الفم. وقيء إلخ : أطلقه فشمل جميع أنواع القيء، سواء قاء من ساعته أم لا. [مراقي الفلاح: ٨٧] إذا ملأ الفم: أي انتقاض الوضوء بأحد هذه الأشياء بشرط كونه ملء الفم. لا ينطبق من الانطباق: انضمام بعضه إلى بعض الأصح يعني ما ذكر من تفسير ملء الفم هو الأصح من التفاسير فيه، وقيل: حده ما يمنع الكلام ويجمع أي إن قاء متفرقاً بحيث لو جمع ملأ الفم، فالمعتبر اتحاد السبب وهو الغثيان وتفسيره : إذا قاء ثانياً قبل سكون النفس من الغثيان فهو متحد، وإن قاء ثانياً بعد سكون النفس فهو مختلف، وهذا هو عند محمد، والمعتبر عند أبي يوسف له اتحاد المجلس. وقال في "مراقي الفلاح" : وقول محمد هو الأصح، وقال أبو علي الدقاق: يجمع كيفما كان [مراقي الفلاح: ۸۹]
And vomit of food, water, blood clot, or bile when it fills the mouth. And vomit etc.: He made it general, including all types of vomit, whether he vomits immediately or not. When it fills the mouth: i.e., the nullification of wudu by one of these things is conditional on it being mouthful. 'It does not close' from closure: joining some to another; the correct [interpretation] - meaning that the mentioned interpretation of 'mouthful' is the most correct of the interpretations. And it is said: its limit is what prevents speech. And he gathers: if he vomits separately such that if gathered it would fill the mouth, then the consideration is unity of cause, which is nausea. Its explanation: if he vomits a second time before the settling of the breath from nausea, it is united; if he vomits a second time after the settling of the breath, it is separate. This is according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, the consideration is unity of sitting. And it is said in Maraqi al-Falah: The statement of Muhammad is the most correct. And Abu Ali al-Daqqaq said: it gathers however it is.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on vomit (qay') as a nullifier of wudu. The main text says vomit of food, water, 'alaq (blood clot), or mirrah (bile) nullifies wudu if it fills the mouth (mil' al-fam). The commentary expands: the ruling applies to all types of vomit, whether immediate or delayed. The condition of 'mouthful' is defined as an amount that, if the mouth is closed, the lips do not naturally meet without effort; the most correct definition is that which prevents normal speech. If a person vomits multiple times, they are considered as one event if the cause (nausea) is continuous, i.e., the second vomit occurs before the nausea subsides. According to Imam Muhammad, if breathing settles between vomits, they are separate. Abu Yusuf considers unity of sitting. The relied-upon view according to Maraqi al-Falah is Muhammad's. Some scholars like Abu Ali al-Daqqaq say any combination counts.

Key Terms
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): Mouthful; an amount of vomit that fills the mouth such that the lips do not close naturally without effort.
اتحاد السبب (ittiḥād al-sabab): Unity of cause; multiple vomits are considered one if from the same bout of nausea.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a person vomits three times in quick succession during the same nausea episode, and each amount is less than a mouthful but together they would fill the mouth, do they nullify wudu?
A) No, because each is less than a mouthful individually.
B) Yes, because they are from one cause and counted together.
C) Yes, only if they occur in one sitting.
D) No, because vomit never nullifies wudu.

Key Takeaway
Vomit nullifies wudu only if it is a mouthful; multiple vomits from one continuous nausea are counted together.

Blood from the Nose: When Does It Nullify Wudu?
Ruling on nasal blood as a wudu nullifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which blood from the nose nullifies wudu according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Nasal blood nullifies wudu only if it reaches the soft inner part.

This lesson focuses on the first part of the passage: the ruling on blood exiting the nose. The key condition is the location it reaches – the soft part (mā lān minhu).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
What is the act being ruled upon? Blood exiting the nose during wudu.
The ruling here is about nullification (naqḍ) of wudu.
2
Identify the condition
What condition triggers nullification? The blood must reach the soft inner part of the nose (al-mā lān minhu).
This refers to the internal fleshy area beyond the bone.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why is the soft part significant? Because it requires purification – it is an internal cavity; blood exiting from there is considered exiting the body.
4
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, determine if nullification occurs. For example, if a nosebleed flows deep into the nose, wudu is broken; if it collects at the nostril tip, it is not.
This is based on whether the blood has crossed the nasal bone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودم غلب على البزاق، أو ساواه. ودم: اعلم أن الدم إذا نزل من الأنف انتقض وضوؤه إذا وصل إلى ما لان منه؛ لأنه يجب تطهيره، وإن خرج من نفس الفم تعتبر الغلبة بينه وبين الريق وإن تساويا انتقض الوضوء؛ لأن البصاق سائل بقوة نفسه، فكذا مساويه، بخلاف المغلوب؛ لأنه سائل بقوة الغالب، ويعتبر ذلك من حيث اللون، فإن كان أحمر انتقض، وإن كان أصفر لا ينتقض، وذكر الإمام علاء الدين أن من أكل خبزاً ورأى أثر الدم فيه من أصول أسنانه، أن ينبغي إصبعه أو طرف كمه على ذلك الموضع، فإن وجد فيه أثر الدم انتقض وضوؤه وإلا فلا. [تبيين الحقائق: ٤٨/١]
And blood that overcomes saliva, or equals it. And blood: Know that if blood descends from the nose, his wudu is nullified when it reaches the soft part [of the nose] because it must be purified. And if it exits from the mouth itself, the predominance between it and saliva is considered; if they are equal, wudu is nullified because saliva is a liquid by its own strength, and thus its equal [is the same], unlike the overcome [saliva] because it is a liquid by the strength of the dominant [substance]. And that is considered based on color: if it is red, it nullifies; if yellow, it does not. And Imam Ala al-Din mentioned that if someone eats bread and sees a trace of blood from the roots of his teeth, he should press his finger or sleeve edge on that spot; if he finds a trace of blood on it, his wudu is nullified, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on blood (dam) as a nullifier. If blood comes from the nose, wudu is nullified if it reaches the part of the nose that is soft (the inner fleshy part), because that area requires purification after bleeding. If blood comes from the mouth (e.g., from gums), the ruling depends on whether the blood overcomes saliva or equals it. If the amount of blood is greater than saliva (ghalaba) or equal (musawat), wudu is nullified. If the blood is less (maghlub), it does not nullify because then the liquid is considered overpowered by saliva. The determination is by color: red indicates blood predominant (nullifies), yellow indicates saliva predominant (does not nullify). A practical application: after eating bread, if one sees blood from the gums and presses the area, if blood appears on the finger, wudu is nullified.

Key Terms
الغالب والمغلوب (al-ghālib wa al-maghlūb): The predominant and the subdued; used to determine whether blood or saliva is more in the mixture.
اللون (al-lawn): Color; red indicates blood dominance, yellow indicates saliva dominance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, blood from the nose nullifies wudu only if:
A) it exits from any part of the nose.
B) it reaches the soft part (mā lān minhu) of the nose.
C) it is a large quantity.
D) it is accompanied by pain.

Key Takeaway
Nasal blood nullifies wudu only if it reaches the soft inner part of the nose.

Blood from the Mouth: The Ghalaba (Predominance) Rule
Ruling on oral blood nullification based on predominance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ghalaba rule to determine whether blood from the mouth nullifies wudu.

Core Idea
Oral blood nullifies wudu only if it overcomes or equals saliva.

This lesson analyzes the rule for blood exiting the mouth. The key is comparing blood to saliva using color as a practical indicator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
What is the act? Blood exiting from the mouth (e.g., from gums).
This is separate from nasal blood; different conditions apply.
2
Understand the ghalaba rule
The ruling depends on whether the blood overcomes (ghalaba) or equals (musawat) the saliva mixed with it.
If blood is less (maghlub), it does not nullify.
3
Apply the color test
The practical method: if the mixture is red, blood dominates; if yellow, saliva dominates.
This is a layperson's tool to determine predominance.
4
Note the practical example
Imam Ala al-Din gives a case: after eating bread, if blood is seen from tooth roots, pressing the spot and seeing blood on the finger indicates nullification.
This illustrates applying the rule when blood is not visibly mixed with saliva.

When Does Sleep Nullify Wudu? Position and Depth
Ruling and conditions for sleep nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which sleep nullifies wudu according to the Hanafi school and apply the distinction between heavy and light drowsiness to determine nullification.

Core Idea
Sleep nullifies wudu only when the sleeper is not firmly seated and the sleep is deep.

The question of whether sleep invalidates wudu is not answered with a simple yes or no. The Hanafi school teaches that the answer depends on two variables: the position of the sleeper and the depth of sleep. This passage from Nur al-Idah unpacks these conditions using both consensus and a prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
Sleep is a potential nullifier (naqid) of wudu. The ruling depends on specific conditions, not the mere occurrence of sleep.
This is a conditional nullifier, not an absolute one.
2
Determine the sleeper's position
Check if the sleeper's buttocks are firmly on the ground. If lying down (mudtaji') or sitting on heels (mutawarrik), the buttocks are off the ground → nullification. If sitting firmly (e.g., on the floor with buttocks planted), sleep does not nullify.
Leaning on a support: if buttocks are off the ground, nullifies; if on ground, does not.
3
Assess the depth of sleep
Distinguish between heavy drowsiness (nu'as thaqil) and light drowsiness (nu'as khafif). The test: if the sleeper can hear what is said near him, it is light; if not, it is heavy. Heavy drowsiness in a prone position nullifies; light drowsiness does not.
This test applies when the person is in a position that would normally nullify.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Quduri and al-Tahawi held that sleep always nullifies wudu regardless of position. The correct (sahih) view is the conditional one, accepted by later authorities like al-Marghinani.
The mufta bihi is the conditional view.
5
Apply to a scenario
Take a person praying in a firm seated position: if they fall asleep lightly (hearing the imam), wudu remains valid. If they lie down and cannot hear anything, wudu is nullified.
The hadith exempts standing/bowing/prostrating positions in prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونوم. ونوم: اعلم أن النائم لا يخلو : إما أن يكون مضطجعاً فينتقض وضوؤه أو متوركاً، وهو ملحق به؛ لزوال المقعدة من الأرض، أو مستنداً إلى شيء لو أزيل عنه لسقط، فهذا لا يخلو إما أن تكون مقعدته زائلة عن الأرض أو لا، فإن كانت زائلة نقض بالإجماع، وإن كانت غير زائلة فقد ذكر القدوري أنه ينتقض وهو مروي عن الطحاوي، والصحيح أنه لا ينتقض أو يكون قائماً أو راكعاً أو ساجداً، فإنه إن كان في الصلاة لا ينتقض وضوؤه؛ لقوله ل: "لا وضوء على من نام قائما أو راكعا أو ساجدا" وإن كان خارج الصلاة فكذلك في الصحيح إن كان لم تتمكن فيه المقعدة من الأرض وارتفاع مقعدة نائم قبل انتباهه، وإن لم يسقط في أي استيقاظه وصلية الظاهر، والمذهب: وإغماء، وجنون، وسكر وقهقهة بالغ يقظان في صلاة ذات ركوع وسجود ولو تعمد الخروج بها من الصلاة ومس فرج بذكر منتصب بلا حائل. مرتكب القهقهة على هيئة السجود بأن كان رافعاً بطنه عن فخذيه محافياً عضديه عن جنبيه، وإلا انتقض وضوؤه، واختلفوا في المريض إذا كان يصلي مضطجعاً فنام فالصحيح أن وضوءه ينتقض؛ لما روينا، والنعاس نوعان: ثقيل وهو حدث في حالة الاضطجاع، وخفيف وهو ليس بحدث فيها والفاصل بينهما إن كان يسمع ما قيل عنده فهو خفيف وإلا فهو ثقيل [تبيين الحقائق: ٥٢/١، ٥٣] لم تتمكن باضطجاع وتورك واستلقاء على القفا. [مراقي الفلاح ۹۰]
And sleep. And sleep: Know that the sleeper is either lying down, so his wudu is nullified; or sitting on his heels (mutawarrikan), which is annexed to it because the buttocks are removed from the ground; or leaning on something such that if removed he would fall. This is either with his buttocks removed from the ground, then it nullifies by consensus, or not removed. Al-Quduri mentioned it nullifies, and it is narrated from al-Tahawi, but the correct view is that it does not nullify. Or he is standing, bowing, or prostrating: if in prayer, his wudu is not nullified because of his saying: 'No wudu is required from one who sleeps standing, bowing, or prostrating.' And if outside prayer, similarly in the correct view if his buttocks are not firmly on the ground. And the elevation of the buttocks of a sleeper before his awakening, even if he does not fall, according to the apparent [view] of the school. And fainting, madness, intoxication, and loud laughter of a conscious adult in a prayer that has bowing and prostration, even if he intentionally exits the prayer by it, and touching the penis with the hand without a barrier. One who commits loud laughter in the posture of prostration, raising his belly from his thighs and separating his arms from his sides, otherwise his wudu is nullified. They differed concerning the sick person who prays lying down and falls asleep: the correct view is that his wudu is nullified because of what we narrated. And drowsiness is of two types: heavy, which is an event (hadath) in the lying position, and light, which is not an event in it. The distinction between them is if he hears what is said near him, it is light; otherwise heavy. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 52-53] Not firmly by lying down, sitting on heels, or lying on the back. [Maraqi al-Falah: 90]
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on sleep (nawm) as a nullifier of wudu. The key factor is whether the sleeper's buttocks are firmly on the ground. If the person is lying down (mudtaji'), sleep nullifies wudu. Sitting on the heels (mutawarrikan) is similar because the buttocks are off the ground. If leaning on something such that removal would cause a fall, if the buttocks are off the ground, nullification is by consensus. If the buttocks are on the ground (like sitting firmly), the correct view is that sleep does not nullify, contrary to al-Quduri and al-Tahawi. For a person standing, bowing, or prostrating, if in prayer, a hadith exempts them: 'No wudu upon one who sleeps standing, bowing, or prostrating.' Outside prayer, similarly if the buttocks are not firmly on the ground? Actually the text says 'if his buttocks are not firmly on the ground' it nullifies. The discussion includes fainting, madness, intoxication, and laughter (which will be in next lessons). Also drowsiness (nu'as) is distinguished: heavy drowsiness (where one does not hear what is said) is a nullifier; light drowsiness (where one hears) is not. For a sick person praying lying down, sleep nullifies wudu because the buttocks are not firmly on the ground.

Key Terms
مضطجع (muḍṭaji'): Lying down on the side or back; sleep in this position nullifies wudu.
متورك (mutawarrik): Sitting on the heels (like in tashahhud); the buttocks are off the ground, so sleep nullifies.
نعاس (nu'ās): Drowsiness; heavy drowsiness (loss of awareness) nullifies, light drowsiness (awareness of surroundings) does not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person prays 'Isha sitting on the ground with buttocks firmly on the floor and falls asleep lightly, hearing the imam. Does his wudu nullify?
A) Yes, because he is asleep.
B) No, because his buttocks are firmly on the ground and he is aware of sounds.
C) Yes, because he is in prayer.
D) No, because sleep only nullifies outside prayer.

Key Takeaway
Sleep nullifies wudu only when the sleeper is not firmly seated and the sleep is deep (heavy drowsiness).

Nullifiers: Fainting, Madness, Intoxication
Ruling and commentary on nullifiers of wudu with disputed definition for intoxication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three nullifiers of wudu related to loss of consciousness and identify the relied-upon Hanafi definition of intoxication that nullifies wudu.

Core Idea
Fainting, madness, and intoxication nullify wudu; unsteady gait is the Hanafi threshold for intoxication.

In this lesson, we examine three additional nullifiers of wudu: fainting, madness, and intoxication. These all involve a loss of consciousness or reason. The passage defines each and clarifies the disputed definition of intoxication that triggers nullification. Understanding this dispute is crucial for applying the correct ruling in everyday situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three nullifiers
List the three conditions mentioned: fainting (ighmā'), madness (junūn), and intoxication (sukr). They all disrupt awareness.
2
Understand the definitions
Fainting is an illness that removes strength and covers the intellect (temporary). Madness is an illness that removes intellect but increases strength (chronic).
These definitions help distinguish the level of consciousness loss.
3
Examine the dispute on intoxication
Two views: some say the hadd penalty definition (cannot distinguish man from woman); others say unsteady gait. The text favors the latter.
The hadd definition is stricter; the relied-upon view is broader.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The correct view (according to Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani) is that intoxication nullifies when the person's walk becomes unsteady.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
5
Apply the ruling
If a person is slightly intoxicated but walks steadily, wudu remains valid. Once gait is unsteady, wudu is nullified.
This distinguishes between mild and significant intoxication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإغماء، وجنون، وسكر. وإغماء وهو مرض يزيل القوى ويستر العقل، والجنون: مرض يزيل العقل ويزيد القوى [مراقي الفلاح: ۹۱] وحد السكر الناقض فيه خلاف، فقيل هو حده في الحد، وهو أن لا يعرف الرجل من المرأة عند بعض المشايخ، وهو اختيار الصدر الشهيد، والصحيح ما قيل عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني: أنه دخل في مشيته تحرك، فهذا سكر ينتقض به الوضوء. [ حاشية الشلبي على تبيين الحقائق: ٥٤/١]
And fainting, madness, and intoxication. And fainting: a disease that removes strength and covers the intellect. Madness: a disease that removes the intellect and increases strength. [Maraqi al-Falah: 91] And the limit of intoxication that nullifies is subject to difference. It is said its definition is that of the hadd penalty: that he does not know a man from a woman according to some scholars, and that is the choice of al-Sadr al-Shahid. And the correct [view] is what is narrated from Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani: that he becomes unsteady in his gait, so that is intoxication which nullifies wudu. [Hashiyat al-Shalabi on Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 54]
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage lists three more nullifiers: fainting (ighma'), madness (junun), and intoxication (sukr). The commentary defines fainting as an illness that removes physical strength and covers the intellect (temporary loss of consciousness). Madness is an illness that removes the intellect but increases physical strength (permanent or chronic). For intoxication, the definition that triggers nullification differs: some say it is the definition for hadd punishment (cannot distinguish a man from a woman), but the relied-upon view (according to Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani) is when the person's gait becomes unsteady, indicating loss of control. So, any level of intoxication that causes unsteadiness nullifies wudu. These three conditions all involve a complete or significant loss of awareness, so wudu is nullified by consensus or by analogy to sleep.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā'): Fainting; a temporary loss of consciousness due to illness.
جنون (junūn): Madness; permanent loss of reason, often with increased physical strength.
سكر (sukr): Intoxication; a state where one loses control due to alcohol or drugs. The nullifying level is when gait becomes unsteady.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view in the passage, which of the following is the criterion for intoxication nullifying wudu?
A) The person cannot recite the Quran.
B) The person cannot distinguish a man from a woman.
C) The person's gait becomes unsteady.
D) The person falls asleep.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view is that intoxication nullifies wudu when the person's gait becomes unsteady, not when they reach the hadd penalty threshold.

Loud Laughter in Prayer: Nullification of Wudu
Ruling and commentary on loud laughter in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on loud laughter (qahqaha) in prayer and distinguish it from ordinary laughter (dahk) and smiling (tabassum), and apply the conditions that determine whether wudu is nullified.

Core Idea
Loud laughter nullifies wudu only in a prayer with ruku and sujud by a conscious adult.

This lesson clarifies a distinctive Hanafi ruling: loud laughter (qahqaha) during prayer not only invalidates the prayer but also nullifies wudu. This is unlike ordinary laughter (dahk) or smiling (tabassum). We will explore the precise conditions and exceptions as laid out in the Nur al-Idah commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage rules about loud laughter in prayer. The main ruling is that qahqaha nullifies wudu and invalidates the prayer.
2
Distinguish types of laughter
Differentiate qahqaha (loud, heard by others) from dahk (ordinary, heard only by self) and tabassum (silent). Only qahqaha nullifies wudu.
3
List the conditions for nullification
Identify the four conditions from the text: the person must be an adult (baligh), conscious (yaqzan), the prayer must normally have ruku and sujud (like five daily prayers, including ima'), and the laughter must occur during the prayer.
4
Note the exceptions
The text excludes children, sleeping persons, prayer outside (like funeral, sajda tilawa), and laughter outside prayer. Also, if qahqaha occurs after final tashahhud but before salam, wudu is nullified but prayer is valid.
5
Apply the ruling to scenarios
Use the conditions to determine whether wudu is nullified in given cases. For example, a conscious adult laughing loudly in Dhuhr prayer: nullifies wudu. A sleeping person laughing: does not nullify wudu.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقهقهة بالغ يقظان في صلاة ذات ركوع وسجود ولو تعمد الخروج بها من الصلاة. وقهقهة: القهقهة ما يكون مسموعاً له و لجيرانه بدت أسنانه أو لا، والضحك ما يكون مسموعاً له دون جيرانه، وهو مبطل للصلاة دون الوضوء، والتبسم مالا صوت فيه ولا تأثير له في واحد منها. أطلق القهقهة فشملت ما إذا كان عمداً أو سهواً، وقيدها بـ"البالغ" فاحترز بها عن الصبي؛ فإن قهقهة الصبي لا تبطل وضوؤه، وب" اليقظان"؛ فإن قهقهة نائم في الصلاة لا تنقض وضوءه على الأصح، لكن تبطل صلاته، وبا " الصلاة"؛ فإن قهقهة بالغ غير نائم خارج الصلاة لا تنقض الوضوء، وبـ"كون الصلاة ذات ركوع وسجود"، فاحترز بها عن صلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة؛ فإن القهقهة فيهما لا تنقض الوضوء. والمراد بذات ركوع وسجود ما إذا كانت بالأصالة ولو لم تكن ذات ركوع وسجود بالفعل لتشمل ما إذا كانت بالإيماء، وأطلق الصلاة فشملت ما إذا كانت حكماً كما إذا قهقه في السهو أو من سبقه الحدث بعد الوضوء قبل أن يبني. ولو تعمد إلخ: أي إذا قهقه مصل مذكور بعد الجلوس الأخير، ولم يبق إلا السلام، ينتقض وضوؤه؛ لوجودها في تحريمة الصلاة، ولكن الصلاة صحيحة لتمام فروضها، وترك واجب السلام لا يمنعه [مراقي الفلاح: ٩٢]
And loud laughter of a conscious adult in a prayer that has bowing and prostration, even if he intentionally exits the prayer by it. And loud laughter: loud laughter is that which is heard by him and his neighbors, whether his teeth appear or not. And laughter (dahk) is that which is heard by him only, and it invalidates the prayer but not wudu. And a smile (tabassum) has no sound and has no effect on either. He made loud laughter general, including whether intentional or forgetful. He restricted it with 'adult' to exclude the child: the loud laughter of a child does not nullify his wudu. And with 'conscious' to exclude the sleeper: the loud laughter of a sleeping person in prayer does not nullify his wudu according to the most correct view, but it invalidates his prayer. And with 'prayer' to exclude the loud laughter of a conscious adult outside prayer: it does not nullify wudu. And with 'it is a prayer that has bowing and prostration' to exclude the funeral prayer and prostration of recitation: loud laughter in them does not nullify wudu. What is meant by 'that has bowing and prostration' is that it is originally so, even if it actually does not have bowing and prostration, to include prayer by gesture (ima'). And he made 'prayer' general to include prayer by ruling, as when one laughs during forgetfulness or when preceded by hadath after wudu before building. 'Even if he intentionally exits': i.e., if the aforementioned worshipper laughs loudly after the final sitting and nothing remains but the salutation, his wudu is nullified because it occurred within the sacredness of the prayer, but the prayer is valid because its pillars are complete, and leaving the obligatory salutation does not prevent that.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on loud laughter (qahqaha) in prayer. The main text states that loud laughter by a conscious adult in a prayer that has bowing and prostration nullifies wudu, even if he intends to exit the prayer by it. The commentary defines qahqaha as laughter that is heard by both the laugher and his neighbors (audible to others), while ordinary laughter (dahk) is heard only by himself and invalidates the prayer but not wudu. A smile (tabassum) has no sound and affects neither. The conditions: the person must be an adult (child's laughter does not nullify wudu), conscious (sleeping person's laughter does not nullify wudu but invalidates the prayer), and the prayer must be one that normally has bowing and prostration (like the five daily prayers, including prayer by gesture, but not funeral prayer or prostration of recitation). Also, if the prayer is in a ruling sense, like when someone laughs while building on a previous prayer (like after hadath), it also nullifies. If the laughter occurs after the final sitting (tashahhud) before saying the salutation, it nullifies wudu because the prayer is still ongoing (in tahrimah), but the prayer itself is valid because all pillars are complete; only the obligatory salutation remains, and missing it does not invalidate the prayer.

Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Loud laughter that is audible to others; it nullifies wudu and invalidates the prayer.
ضحك (ḍaḥk): Ordinary laughter heard only by the laugher; invalidates prayer but does not nullify wudu.
تبسم (tabassum): Smile; no sound, no effect on prayer or wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on loud laughter (qahqaha) in the funeral prayer (janazah)?
A) It nullifies wudu and invalidates the prayer.
B) It nullifies wudu but does not invalidate the prayer.
C) It does not nullify wudu because the funeral prayer does not have bowing and prostration.
D) It nullifies wudu only if intentional.

Key Takeaway
Only loud laughter (qahqaha) by a conscious adult in a prayer with ruku and sujud nullifies wudu; other laughter types and conditions do not.

Touching the Genitals: Conditions for Nullifying Wudu
Ruling and expansion on touching genitals as a wudu nullifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which touching the private part nullifies wudu in the Hanafi school, and distinguish between a thick and thin barrier.

Core Idea
Touching the erect genital with bare skin or a thin cloth nullifies wudu — only a heat-blocking barrier saves it.

This passage from Nur al-Idah explains a key nullifier of wudu: touching the private part. The ruling is not as simple as it seems — it depends on the state of the organ (erect), the type of contact, and the presence of a barrier. The commentary clarifies that the barrier must be thick enough to prevent body heat, otherwise wudu is still broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled
The main text states: 'Touching the penis with an erect penis without a barrier' nullifies wudu. Identify that the act is touching one's own private part with the hand (or equivalent).
The term 'private part' (faraj) includes penis and vulva.
2
Determine the conditions for nullification
The ruling applies when the private part is erect (for men) and the touch occurs without a thick barrier. The commentary expands: touching the anus, penis-to-penis (between two men), or vulva-to-vulva (two women) also nullifies.
Erection is a condition; touching a flaccid penis does not nullify wudu, according to the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the barrier condition
The phrase 'without a barrier' is clarified: a barrier that prevents body heat (i.e., thick) prevents nullification; a thin barrier that transmits heat does not. So touching with bare skin or a thin cloth nullifies.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
4
Apply to new cases
The commentary provides examples: direct contact between two men's genitals or two women's genitals also nullifies wudu. This expands the scope beyond manual touch.
These cases are considered by analogy.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
While Hanafis consider this a nullifier, Shafi'is do not — they hold that touching the private part does not nullify wudu, only invalidates prayer if done intentionally.
This difference affects practical rulings in daily life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومس فرج بذكر منتصب بلا حائل. ومس فرج إلخ: اعلم أن قيد الفرج اتفاقي، فإن مس الدبر بالذكر أو مس الذكر بالذكر كما في مباشرة الرجلين أو مس الفرج بالفرج كما في مباشرة المرأتين ناقضة أيضاً. بلا حائل نفى الحائل مط مطلقاً، وهو مقيد بحائل يمنع حرارة الجسد؛ لئلا يرد عليه حائل رقيق لا يمنع الحرارة، فإن الوضوء ينتقض في الحالتين، سواء لم يكن حائل أصلاً أو كان رقيقاً لا يمنع الحرارة.
And touching the penis with an erect penis without a barrier. And touching the private part, etc.: Know that the restriction of 'private part' is agreed upon, for touching the anus with the penis, or touching the penis with the penis as in direct contact of two men, or touching the private part with the private part as in direct contact of two women, is also nullifying. Without a barrier: he negates the barrier absolutely, and it is restricted to a barrier that prevents body heat, so that a thin barrier that does not prevent heat does not come under it; for wudu is nullified in both cases: whether there is no barrier at all or it is thin and does not prevent heat.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on touching the genitals (mas al-faraj) as a nullifier. The main text says: touching the erect penis with the hand (or other part?) without a barrier nullifies wudu. The commentary clarifies that 'private part' (faraj) is not limited to the penis; touching the anus with the penis, touching penis to penis (as in physical contact of two men), or vulva to vulva (two women) also nullifies. The condition of 'without a barrier' is absolute in the matn but the commentary restricts it: a barrier that prevents body heat (i.e., a thick barrier) prevents nullification; a thin barrier that transmits heat does not prevent nullification. So if one touches with a thin cloth, wudu is still nullified.

Key Terms
مس الفرج (mas al-faraj): Touching the private part (penis or vulva) with the hand; here specifically with an erect penis (for men) and without a thick barrier.
حائل (ḥā'il): Barrier; a thick barrier that prevents body heat sufficing to prevent nullification. A thin barrier (like cloth) does not prevent nullification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wudu remains valid.
B) Wudu becomes nullified.
C) Wudu becomes nullified only if he intends to.
D) Wudu becomes nullified only if he touches it from the front.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, touching the genital with an erect organ through a thin barrier nullifies wudu; only a thick heat-blocking barrier prevents nullification.

Loss of Consciousness as Nullifiers: Sleep, Unconsciousness, Madness
Listing and explaining sleep, unconsciousness, and madness as nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which sleep, unconsciousness, and madness nullify wudu according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Sleep only nullifies wudu if it is deep (heavy) and in a reclining position; unconsciousness and madness always nullify.

This lesson focuses on three nullifiers of wudu that involve loss of consciousness: sleep, unconsciousness (ighma), and madness (junun). The Hanafi school distinguishes between heavy and light sleep, and between conscious and unconscious states. We will examine the definitions and conditions that determine when wudu is nullified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nullifiers
Recognize that sleep, unconsciousness, and madness are listed as nullifiers in the opening line of the passage.
The passage begins by mentioning the condition of the buttocks not being firmly on the ground, which relates to sleep.
2
Understand the sleeping position
Note that wudu is nullified if the buttocks lose firm contact with the ground while sleeping, even slightly before waking.
This includes lying on the side, sitting with thighs apart, or lying on the back.
3
Distinguish heavy and light sleep
Apply the test: if the person hears sounds around him, it is light sleep; if not, it is heavy sleep. Heavy sleep in a reclining position nullifies wudu.
There is a difference of opinion on the sick person who prays lying down and then sleeps; the correct view is nullification.
4
Define unconsciousness and madness
Unconsciousness (ighma) is a disease that removes bodily strength and conceals the intellect. Madness (junun) removes intellect and increases strength.
Both are considered absolute nullifiers with no further conditions.
5
Apply the rulings
Given a scenario, determine whether wudu is nullified based on the conditions: for sleep, check heaviness and position; for ighma and junun, nullification is automatic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نواقض الوضوء: الأرض لم تتمكن فيه المقعدة من الأرض وارتفاع مقعدة نائم قبل انتباهه، وإن لم يسقط في أي استيقاظه وصلية الظاهر، وإغماء، وجنون، وسكر وقهقهة بالغ يقظان في صلاة ذات ركوع وسجود ولو تعمد الخروج بها من الصلاة ومس فرج بذكر منتصب بلا حائل. مرتكب القهقهة على هيئة السجود بأن كان رافعاً بطنه عن فخذيه محافياً عضديه عن جنبيه، وإلا انتقض وضوؤه، واختلفوا في المريض إذا كان يصلي مضطجعاً فنام فالصحيح أن وضوءه ينتقض؛ لما روينا، والنعاس نوعان: ثقيل وهو حدث في حالة الاضطجاع، وخفيف وهو ليس بحدث فيها والفاصل بينهما إن كان يسمع ما قيل عنده فهو خفيف وإلا فهو ثقيل. تبيين الحقائق: ٥٢/١، ٥٣] لم تتمكن باضطجاع وتورك واستلقاء على القفا. مراقي الفلاح ۹۰] في الظاهر أي حكم انتقاض وضوئه بمجرد ارتفاع مقعدته قبل الانتباه في الظاهر من المذهب وإغماء وهو مرض يزيل القوى ويستر العقل، والجنون: مرض يزيل العقل ويزيد القوى مراقي الفلاح: ۹۱] وحد السكر الناقض فيه خلاف، فقيل هو حده في الحد، وهو أن لا يعرف الرجل من المرأة عند بعض المشايخ، وهو اختيار الصدر الشهيد، والصحيح ما قيل عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني: أنه دخل في مشيته تحرك، فهذا سكر ينتقض به الوضوء. [ حاشية الشلبي على تبيين الحقائق: ٥٤/١] وقهقهة: القهقهة ما يكون مسموعاً له و لجيرانه بدت أسنانه أو لا، والضحك ما يكون مسموعاً له دون جيرانه، وهو مبطل للصلاة دون الوضوء، والتبسم مالا صوت فيه ولا تأثير له في واحد منها أطلق القهقهة فشملت ما إذا كان عمداً أو سهواً، وقيدها بـ"البالغ" فاحترز بها عن الصبي؛ فإن قهقهة الصبي لا تبطل وضوؤه، وب" اليقظان"؛ فإن قهقهة نائم في الصلاة لا تنقض وضوءه على الأصح، لكن تبطل صلاته، وبا " الصلاة"؛ فإن قهقهة بالغ غير نائم خارج الصلاة لا تنقض الوضوء، وبـ"كون الصلاة ذات ركوع وسجود"، فاحترز بها عن صلاة الجنازة وسجدة التلاوة؛ فإن القهقهة فيهما لا تنقض الوضوء. والمراد بذات ركوع وسجود ما إذا كانت بالأصالة ولو لم تكن ذات ركوع وسجود بالفعل لتشمل ما إذا كانت بالإيماء، وأطلق الصلاة فشملت ما إذا كانت حكماً كما إذا قهقه في السهو أو من سبقه الحدث بعد الوضوء قبل أن ولو تعمد إلخ: أي إذا قهقه مصل مذكور بعد الجلوس الأخير، ولم يبق إلا السلام، ينتقض وضوؤه؛ لوجودها يبني. في تحريمة الصلاة، ولكن الصلاة صحيحة لتمام فروضها، وترك واجب السلام لا يمنعه [مراقي الفلاح: ٩٢] ومس فرج إلخ: اعلم أن قيد الفرج اتفاقي، فإن مس الدبر بالذكر أو مس الذكر بالذكر كما في مباشرة الرجلين أو مس الفرج بالفرج كما في مباشرة المرأتين ناقضة أيضاً. بلا حائل نفى الحائل مط مطلقاً، وهو مقيد بحائل يمنع حرارة الجسد؛ لئلا يرد عليه حائل رقيق لا يمنع الحرارة، فإن الوضوء ينتقض في الحالتين، سواء لم يكن حائل أصلاً أو كان رقيقاً لا يمنع الحرارة.
Nullifiers of wudu: [When] the earth is not firmly seated [for a person] so that his buttocks are not on the ground, and the raising of the buttocks of a sleeping person before he wakes up, even if he does not fall in his awakening—the apparent [ruling]; and unconsciousness, madness, drunkenness, and loud laughter of a mature, wakeful person in a prayer that has bowing and prostration, even if he deliberately intends to exit the prayer thereby; and touching the penis with an erect penis without a barrier. The one who laughs loudly in the posture of prostration by raising his belly from his thighs and keeping his upper arms away from his sides—otherwise his wudu is nullified. They differed about a sick person who prays lying down and then sleeps; the correct [view] is that his wudu is nullified, because of what we narrated. Sleep is of two types: heavy—it is a hadath in the lying down state; and light—it is not a hadath in it. The distinction between them is: if he hears what is said near him, it is light; otherwise it is heavy. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 1/52, 53] Not firmly seated by lying on the side, sitting with thighs apart, or lying on the back. [Maraqi al-Falah: 90] In the apparent [meaning]: i.e., the ruling that his wudu is nullified merely by the raising of his buttocks before awakening in the apparent [view] of the madhhab. Unconsciousness: a disease that removes strength and conceals the intellect. Madness: a disease that removes the intellect and increases strength. [Maraqi al-Falah: 91] The limit of nullifying drunkenness is disputed. It is said: its limit in the [penalty for] intoxication is that he does not know a man from a woman, according to some mashayikh; this is the choice of al-Sadr al-Shahid. The correct [view] is what is related from Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani: that he is unsteady in his gait—this is drunkenness that nullifies wudu. [Hashiyat al-Shalabi 'ala Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 1/54] And loud laughter: loud laughter (qahqaha) is that which is audible to him and his neighbors, whether his teeth appear or not. Laughter (dahk) is that which is audible to him but not to his neighbors; it nullifies prayer but not wudu. A smile (tabassum) has no sound and no effect on either. [The term] qahqaha is unrestricted, so it includes whether intentional or forgetful. It is restricted by 'mature' to exclude a child; the qahqaha of a child does not nullify his wudu. And by 'wakeful'—the qahqaha of a sleeping person in prayer does not nullify his wudu according to the most correct [view], but it does nullify his prayer. And by 'prayer'—the qahqaha of a mature, non-sleeping person outside prayer does not nullify wudu. And by 'prayer that has bowing and prostration'—to exclude the funeral prayer and the prostration of recitation; qahqaha in these does not nullify wudu. The meaning of 'having bowing and prostration' is that it is originally so, even if it is not actually with bowing and prostration, to include [prayer] by gesture. 'Prayer' is unrestricted, so it includes [prayer] by ruling, as when one laughs out loud during a forgetfulness [prostration] or when his hadath occurs after wudu before building [on the prayer]... And even if he deliberately exits, etc.: i.e., if the aforementioned praying person laughs out loud after the final sitting, and nothing remains except the salutation, his wudu is nullified because the qahqaha occurred while in the state of being bound by the prayer's beginning. But the prayer is valid because its pillars are complete, and leaving the obligatory salutation does not prevent [validity]. [Maraqi al-Falah: 92] And touching the penis, etc.: Know that the restriction to the penis is by agreement; indeed, touching the anus with the penis, or touching the penis with the penis as in the case of two men touching, or touching the vulva with the vulva as in the case of two women touching, is also nullifying. Without a barrier: 'without a barrier' is unrestricted, but it is qualified by a barrier that prevents the heat of the body; otherwise a thin barrier that does not prevent heat would be an objection. Wudu is nullified in both cases: whether there is no barrier at all or there is a thin barrier that does not prevent heat.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Nur al-Idah, listing the nullifiers of wudu and then expanded with commentary from later works like Tabyin al-Haqa'iq and Maraqi al-Falah. The author first states the nullifiers: (1) a person's buttocks not being firmly on the ground while sleeping, leading to loss of control; (2) unconsciousness; (3) madness; (4) drunkenness; (5) loud laughter in a prayer that has bowing and prostration; and (6) touching the penis with an erect penis without a barrier. The commentary then elaborates on each. For the first, it explains that even if the sleeping person's buttocks rise slightly before waking, wudu is nullified according to the apparent madhhab, and this applies whether the person is lying on the side, sitting with thighs apart (tawarruk), or lying on the back. There is a difference of opinion regarding a sick person praying lying down who then falls asleep; the correct view is that wudu is nullified because of a narration. Sleep is divided into heavy and light: heavy sleep nullifies wudu in the lying down state, light does not; the test is whether the person hears what is said near him. For unconsciousness and madness, definitions are given: unconsciousness removes strength and conceals intellect; madness removes intellect and increases strength. For drunkenness, the nullifying limit is disputed: some say it is when one cannot distinguish a man from a woman (the view of al-Sadr al-Shahid), but the correct view according to Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani is that it is unsteady gait. For loud laughter (qahqaha), it is distinguished from laughter (dahk) and smiling (tabassum). Qahqaha is audible to the person and his neighbors; it nullifies both wudu and prayer if it occurs in a prayer that has bowing and prostration, provided the person is mature, wakeful, and in such a prayer. If the person is a child or asleep, qahqaha does not nullify wudu. Qahqaha outside prayer does not nullify wudu. Qahqaha in funeral prayer or prostration of recitation does not nullify wudu. If qahqaha occurs after the final sitting before saying salaam, wudu is nullified but the prayer is valid because its pillars are complete. For touching the penis (mas farj), the restriction to the penis is by agreement; the same ruling applies to touching the anus with the penis, or two men's penises touching, or two women's vulvas touching. The requirement of 'no barrier' is qualified: a thin barrier that does not prevent heat does not prevent nullification; wudu is nullified whether there is no barrier or a thin barrier that transmits heat. This passage is dense with specific conditions and intra-madhhab differences, and it follows the typical Hanafi method of listing rulings then expanding with proofs and definitions.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā’): A disease that removes bodily strength and conceals intellect; a nullifier of wudu by agreement.
جنون (junūn): A disease that removes intellect and increases bodily strength; nullifies wudu.
نعاس (nu‘ās): Sleep; divided into heavy (thaqil) and light (khafif) based on whether one hears sounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) If the person is lying on his back.

C) If the person is sick.
D) If the person is in a state of prayer.

Key Takeaway
Sleep nullifies wudu only when it is heavy (no hearing) and in a position where the buttocks are not firmly grounded; unconsciousness and madness always nullify.

Ten Things That Do Not Break Wudu
Lists ten actions that do not invalidate wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten items that do not break wudu according to the Hanafi school and explain the distinction between flowing and non-flowing impurities.

Core Idea
Only what flows breaks wudu—blood, flesh, and touch without flow are harmless.

The Hanafi school is precise in defining what nullifies wudu. While other schools may consider certain touches or bleeding as breaking wudu, Imam Abu Hanifa and his students identified exactly ten things that do NOT break it. This lesson presents that list verbatim from Nur al-Idah, a foundational matn in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of each item
Read through the list and group each item into broader categories: body fluids (blood, flesh, worm), touch (penis, woman), vomit (food, phlegm), and sleep (swaying, seated, in prayer).
This helps organize the memorization.
2
Note the qualifying condition for blood and flesh
Blood does not break wudu unless it flows beyond its source. Flesh does not break wudu unless accompanied by flowing blood. The term 'lam yasil' (did not flow) is key.
The Medinan boil (al-'araq al-madani) is an example of flesh without blood flow.
3
Understand the touch rulings
Touching one's own penis or a woman (non-mahram or mahram) does not break wudu in the Hanafi school, contrary to the Shafi'i view.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position.
4
Distinguish vomit types
Vomiting food that fills the mouth breaks wudu; vomiting that does not fill the mouth, or vomiting pure phlegm, does not break wudu regardless of quantity.
The key is whether the vomit is food and its amount.
5
Analyze sleep conditions
Sleep only breaks wudu if the person loses firm seating, such that if support is removed, they would fall. Swaying or sleeping in prayer while maintaining posture does not break wudu.
The criterion is stability of the seat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما لا ينقض الوضوء عشرة أشياء لا تنقض الوضوء : ظهور دم لم يسل عن محله وسقوط لحم من غير سیلان دم كالعرق المدني الذي يقال له رشته وخروج دودة من جرح وأذن وأنف، ومس ذكر، ومس امرأة، وقيء لا يملأ الفم، وقيء بلغم ولو كثيرا، وتمايل نائم احتمل زوال مقعدته ونوم متمكن ولو مستندا إلى شيء لو أزيل سقط على الأرض ونوم مصل ولو راكعا أو ساجدا على جهة السنة، والله الموفق. من مذهب أبي حنيفة
Section on what does not break wudu. Ten things do not break wudu: appearance of blood that did not flow from its place, falling of flesh without blood flow like the Medinan vein called 'rishta', emergence of a worm from a wound, ear, or nose, touching the penis, touching a woman, vomiting that does not fill the mouth, vomiting of phlegm even if plenty, swaying of a sleeping person from whom the removal of his seat is probable, sleeping firmly seated even if leaning on something that if removed he would fall to the ground, and sleeping of a person in prayer even if bowing or prostrating in the sunnah manner. And Allah is the giver of success. From the school of Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating ten specific actions or events that do not break the state of wudu according to the Hanafi school. The author presents them as a list for memorization. The items include: 1) blood that appears but does not flow beyond its source, 2) a piece of flesh detaching without blood flow—like the Medinan boil called 'rishta', 3) a worm emerging from a wound, ear, or nose, 4) touching one's own penis, 5) touching a woman (the Hanafi position is that such touch does not break wudu), 6) vomiting that does not fill the mouth, 7) vomiting phlegm even if copious, 8) a sleeping person swaying such that his seat might shift, 9) sleeping while firmly seated even with support—if the support were removed he would fall, and 10) sleeping of a person in prayer even in bowing or prostration as long as it is in the sunnah manner. The author concludes with 'from the school of Abu Hanifa', indicating these are the chosen positions of the Imam.

Key Terms
لم يسل (lam yasil): Did not flow: blood that appears at the surface but does not move from its place.
العرق المدني (al-'araq al-madani): Medinan boil: a flesh-like protrusion that oozes without blood flow.
مس ذكر (mass dhakar): Touching the penis: does not break wudu according to Abu Hanifa.
مس امرأة (mass imra'ah): Touching a woman: does not break wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nur al-Idah, which of the following does NOT break wudu?
A) Blood that flows from a cut
B) Vomiting that fills the mouth
C) Sleeping while leaning against a wall such that if removed, the person would fall
D) Touching the penis

Key Takeaway
Only impure substances that exit the body and flow, or actions that undermine bodily stability (like unstable sleep), break wudu; the ten listed items are safe.

The Four Explicit Causes of Ghusl
Lists and explains the first four causes that make ghusl obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes of ghusl explicitly mentioned in the text and state the conditions for each according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Ghusl is fard when semen exits with desire, the glans is concealed, or specific intercourse or fluids occur.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lists the causes that make ghusl obligatory. While the text mentions seven causes in total, it elaborates on four. Understanding these causes and their conditions is essential for correctly determining when major ritual purification is required.

Guided Path
1
List the four causes
From the Arabic, identify the four events that obligate ghusl: (1) semen emission with desire, (2) concealment of the glans, (3) semen emission from bestiality or intercourse with a dead body, (4) presence of fluid after sleep.
2
Analyze the conditions for cause 1
Note that 'with desire' is required; gushing is not explicitly required because desire implies it. Also, it includes wet dreams and first time of puberty.
3
Analyze the conditions for cause 2
The glans must be fully concealed; the partner must be a living human of reproductive age; amputees subject to equivalent measure; a young girl who is desirable counts.
4
Analyze cause 3
Emission must occur; mere intercourse with a dead body or animal does not obligate ghusl.
5
Analyze cause 4
The presence of a thin fluid after sleep; the text then launches into a detailed classification (covered in Lesson 2).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في ما يوجب الاغتسال يفترض الغسل بواحد من سبعة أشياء: خروج المني إلى ظاهر الجسد، إذا انفصل عن مقره بشهوة من غير جماع، وتواري حشفة وقدرها من مقطوعها في أحد سبيلي رأس ذكر آدمي حي، وإنزال المني بوطء ميتة أو بهيمة، ووجود ماء كما هو. المعاني الموجبة للغسل مقره: أي مقر المني: وهو الصلب والترائب. (مراقي الفلاح بشهوة: فإن قلت: لم لم يقل الشيخ بشهوة ودفق المشهور عندهم؟ قلنا: أغنى اشتراط الشهوة عن الدفق لملازمته لها قال البيضاوي يحظه: وماء دافق يعني ذا دفق، وهو صب فيه دفع غير جماع: أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان خروج المني من ذكر أو نظر أو عبث أو احتلام، ولو بأول مرة لبلوغ في الأصح، وقيل: لا يجب الغسل بالاحتلام أول مرة لبلوغ؛ لأنه صار والتقييد بقولنا: "لبلوغ" للاحتراز عما إذا تحقق البلوغ أولاً من غير إنزال ثم أنزل، يجب الغسل من . غير خلاف احترز به عن تواري: أي إذا توارت حشفة في قبل أو دبر من آدمي حي إذا كان الذكر سالماً، وإن كان رأس الذكر مقطوعاً وغاب قدر الحشفة في واحد منهما ينتقض الوضوء به أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا غيب الحشفة كلها؛ فإنه إذا غاب أقل منها أو أقل من قدرها من المقطوع، لم يجب الغسل كما في "القهستاني"، والحشفة كما في "القاموس": ما فوق الختان، والمراد بها هنا رأس ذكر، احترز به عن المصنوع من جلد والإصبع، "آدمي احترز به عن ذكر البهائم، "مشتهي" ذكر لا يشتهي والذكر المقطوع، "حي" احترز به عن ذكر الميت والبالغة يوجب عليها تواري حشفة المراهق الغسل. آدمي: أي إذا كان تواري الحشفة في أحد سبيلي حي، فبقولنا: "آدمي" احترز عن غيره كالبهائم والميتة، وأطلق قوله: حياً وهو مقيد بحي يجامع مثله؛ فإنه لا يجب الغسل بالجماع في هذه الأشياء ولا ينتقض الوضوء، وإنما يلزمه غسل ذكره كما في "القهستاني" من "النواقض"، ودخل في قولنا: "حي يجامع مثله" صغيرة تشتهى و لم يفضها؛ لأنها صارت ممن يجامع في الصحيح. إنزال المني إلخ شرط الإنزال ؛ لأن مجرد وطئهما لا يوجب الغسل ولا ينقض الوضوء. [حاشية الطحطاوي ومراقي الفلاح: ٩٩ وجود ماء إلخ : أي من موجبات الغسل وجود ماء رقيق بعد الانتباه من النوم، و حاصل مسألة النوم اثنا عشر وجهاً كما في البحر"؛ لأنه إما أن يتيقن أنه مني أو مذي أو ودي، أو يشك في الأول مع الثاني، أو في الأول الثالث، فهذه ستة، وفي كل منهما إما أن يتذكر احتلاما أو لا، فتمت الاثنا عشر فيجب الغسل اتفاقا فيما إذا تيقن أنه مني تذكر احتلاما أو لا، وكذا فيما إذا تيقن أنه مذي وتذكر الاحتلام أو شك أنه مني أو مذي، أو شك أنه مني أو ودي، أو شك أنه مذي أو ودي وتذكر الاحتلام في الكل، ولا يجب الغسل اتفاقاً فيما إذا تيقن أنه ودي مطلقًا تذكر الاحتلام أو لا، أو شك أنه مذي أو ودي و لم يتذكر أو تيقن أنه مذي ولم يتذكر ويجب الغسل عندهما لا عند أبي يوسف فيما إذا شك أنه مني أو مذي، أو شك أنه مني أو ودي و لم يتذكر احتلاماً فيهما، والمراد بالتيقن هنا غلبة الظن؛ لأن حقيقة اليقين متعذرة مع النوم. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٩٩]
Section on what necessitates ghusl. Ghusl is obligatory by one of seven things: emission of semen to the exterior of the body, when it separates from its place with desire without intercourse; and concealment of the glans and its amount from the amputated part in one of the two passages of the head of a living human male penis; and emission of semen by intercourse with a dead body or an animal; and the presence of water as it is. The meanings that necessitate ghusl: its place: the place of semen: it is the backbone and the collarbones. (Muraqi al-Falah: 'with desire': if you say: why did the shaykh not say with desire and gushing as is famous among them? We say: the condition of desire suffices for gushing because of its inseparability. Al-Baydawi said: 'and gushing water' meaning possessing gush, and it is pouring with force. 'Without intercourse': it is absolute and includes emission of semen by thought, sight, playing, or wet dream, even if for the first time of puberty in the sound opinion; and it is said: ghusl is not obligatory by wet dream the first time of puberty because he becomes... and the restriction by 'for puberty' is to exclude when puberty is realized first without emission then he emits: ghusl is obligatory from it... no difference. He guarded by 'concealment': that is, when the glans disappears in the front or back of a living human, if the penis is sound; and if the head of the penis is amputated and the amount of the glans disappears in either of them, wudu is invalidated by it; it is absolute and restricted by when all of the glans is hidden; if less than it or less than its amount from the amputated disappears, ghusl is not obligatory as in al-Qahistani. And the glans as in al-Qamus: what is above the circumcision, and what is meant here is the head of the penis; he guarded against that which is made of leather and the finger. 'Human' guards against the penis of animals. 'Desired' guards against the penis that is not desired and the amputated penis. 'Living' guards against the penis of a dead person. And a woman who has reached puberty is obliged ghusl by the concealment of the glans of a pubescent boy. 'Human': that is, when the concealment of the glans is in one of the two passages of a living human; by our saying 'human' he guards against other than it like animals and dead bodies; and he said 'living' absolutely and it is restricted to one who mates with his like; for ghusl is not obligatory by intercourse with these things nor does wudu break, and only washing of his penis is required as in al-Qahistani from 'al-Nawaqid'. And included in our saying 'living who mates with his like' is a young girl who is desired and has not been deflowered; because she has become one who mates in the sound view. 'Emission of semen etc.' is a condition for emission; because mere intercourse does not obligate ghusl nor break wudu. [Hashiya al-Tahtawi and Muraqi al-Falah: 99 'Presence of water etc.': that is, from the obligators of ghusl is the presence of thin water after waking up from sleep; and the conclusion of the issue of sleep is twelve aspects as in al-Bahr; because either he is certain that it is semen or madhy or wady, or he doubts between the first and the second, or between the first and the third; these are six, and in each either he remembers a wet dream or not; so twelve are complete. Ghusl is obligatory by agreement when he is certain it is semen, whether he remembers a wet dream or not; likewise when he is certain it is madhy and remembers a wet dream, or he doubts whether it is semen or madhy, or doubts whether it is semen or wady, or doubts whether it is madhy or wady and remembers a wet dream in all. Ghusl is not obligatory by agreement when he is certain it is wady absolutely, whether he remembers a wet dream or not; or doubts whether it is madhy or wady and did not remember; or is certain it is madhy and did not remember. And ghusl is obligatory according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) but not according to Abu Hanifa in cases where he doubts whether it is semen or madhy, or doubts whether it is semen or wady and did not remember a wet dream in both; and what is meant by certainty here is preponderance of opinion, because the reality of certainty is unattainable with sleep. [Hashiya al-Tahtawi: 99]
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the ruling that ghusl (full body wash) becomes obligatory due to seven causes, then it details four of them: (1) emission of semen with desire, even without intercourse; (2) concealment of the glans (or its equivalent in an amputated case) in the front or back passage of a living human; (3) emission of semen from intercourse with a dead body or an animal; and (4) presence of a fluid (water) after sleep. The remainder of the passage is an expansion (sharh) explaining each item. The commentary clarifies that 'with desire' is a necessary condition for the first cause, and that ghusl does not require gushing (دفق) because desire implies it. For the second cause, it details that the glans must be fully concealed, and the partner must be a living human of reproductive age; a young girl who is desirable but not yet deflowered counts. For the third, emission must actually occur; mere intercourse does not obligate ghusl. The fourth cause is discussed in depth regarding the 'water' found after sleep (likely madi or wady); a detailed classification of twelve scenarios based on certainty or doubt about the fluid and recollection of a wet dream is given, with the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. The Hanafi school's reliance on preponderance of opinion (غلبة الظن) due to the difficulty of certainty during sleep is noted. This expansion highlights the precision of Hanafi fiqh in conditional rulings.

Key Terms
المني (al-maniy): Semen: a thick white fluid that exits with desire and break in the penis.
تواري حشفة (tawari hashafah): Concealment of the glans: the complete disappearance of the head of the penis in the vagina or anus of a living human.
الغسل (al-ghusl): Full body wash with intention to remove major ritual impurity (janabah).
التيقن (al-tayaqqu): Certainty or preponderant belief about the nature of fluid after sleep.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following would NOT obligate ghusl?
A) A man finds semen on his clothes after a wet dream.
B) A man has intercourse with a living woman and withdraws before emission.
C) A man's glans is fully inserted into the vagina of a living woman.
D) A man has intercourse with a dead body and semeno emittance.

Key Takeaway
Ghusl is fard upon the occurrence of specific causes: semen emission with desire, glans concealment, bestiality with emission, or finding a fluid after sleep.

The Twelve Aspects and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Doubtful Fluids After Sleep
Provides a detailed classification of twelve scenarios regarding fluid after sleep and an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the twelve scenarios related to fluid found after sleep and state the ruling for each according to the Hanafi school, including the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The ruling on ghusl after sleep depends on certainty or preponderant opinion about the fluid; Abu Hanifa differs from the Sahibayn in two cases of doubt without dream recollection.

The final cause of ghusl discussed in the passage is the presence of a fluid after sleep. The Hanafi scholars have elaborated twelve scenarios based on what fluid is identified and whether a wet dream was remembered. This shows the precision of Hanafi fiqh in conditional rulings and highlights an intra-madhhab disagreement about when doubt requires precaution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis: three fluids
Semen (maniy), pre-seminal fluid (madhy), and post-urine fluid (wady). These have different rules.
2
Learn the six combinations of certainty/doubt
Person is certain it is maniy, certain madhy, certain wady, doubts between maniy and madhy, doubts between maniy and wady, doubts between madhy and wady.
These are the six states of knowledge about the fluid.
3
Add the wet dream variable
For each of the six, either the person remembers a wet dream or does not, making twelve total scenarios.
4
Identify the agreed rulings
Ghusl obligatory when certain of maniy (any case) or certain of madhy with dream recollection, or when doubt between maniy and madhy/madhy and wady with dream. Ghusl not obligatory when certain of wady (any), certain of madhy without dream, or doubt between madhy/wady without dream.
5
Locate the disagreement
In cases of doubt between maniy and madhy OR maniy and wady without dream recollection, Abu Hanifa says no ghusl, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say ghusl is obligatory.
This is the only intra-Hanafi difference in this topic.
6
Apply preponderance of opinion
In sleep, true certainty is impossible; the criterion is ghalabat al-zann (preponderant belief).
The text states: 'المراد بالتيقن هنا غلبة الظن'.

For the second cause, it details that the glans must be fully concealed, and the partner must be a living human of reproductive age; a young girl who is desirable but not yet deflowered counts. For the third, emission must actually occur; mere intercourse does not obligate ghusl. The fourth cause is discussed in depth regarding the 'water' found after sleep (likely madi or wady); a detailed classification of twelve scenarios based on certainty or doubt about the fluid and recollection of a wet dream is given, with the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. The Hanafi school's reliance on preponderance of opinion (غلبة الظن) due to the difficulty of certainty during sleep is noted. This expansion highlights the precision of Hanafi fiqh in conditional rulings.

Key Terms
المني (al-maniy): Semen: requires ghusl when found after sleep with dream or if certain.
المذي (al-madhy): Pre-seminal fluid: requires wudu, not ghusl, unless dream is remembered in certain scenarios.
الودي (al-wady): Thick fluid after urine: does not require wudu or ghusl.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Preponderance of opinion; the standard for certainty when exact knowledge is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario does Abu Hanifa disagree with the Sahibayn?
A) Certain it is semen with dream recollection
B) Certain it is madhy without dream recollection
C) Doubt between semen and madhy without dream recollection
D) Doubt between madhy and wady with dream recollection

Key Takeaway
The ruling on ghusl after sleep is contingent on the type of fluid, the person's knowledge, and dream recollection; a nuanced classification avoids unnecessary obligations while ensuring purification when required.

Three Causes of Ghusl: Conditions and Rulings
Listing causes of ghusl with commentary on conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three causes of ghusl and identify the specific conditions that make each cause trigger the obligation.

Core Idea
Three causes obligate ghusl: maniy with desire, glans insertion in a living human, and emission from corpse/animal — each with distinct conditions.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lays out the foundational causes that make ghusl obligatory (fard 'ayn). Understanding these causes and their conditions is essential for correct practice of purification. The text also clarifies subtle points about the first wet dream at puberty and the requirement that the partner be alive and capable of intercourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes
Read the matn and commentary to extract the three distinct events that obligate ghusl: (1) maniy exiting with desire, (2) covering of the glans in a living human's front or back passage, (3) emission of maniy from intercourse with a corpse or animal.
The text lists these in sequence; note the condition for each.
2
Analyze the condition for maniy
The text specifies 'with desire' (بشهوة) and notes that gushing is not separately required because it is implied. Understand that any exit of maniy with desire, even from memory or wet dream, obligates ghusl.
The weak opinion on first wet dream is mentioned; the relied-upon view is that ghusl is required.
3
Analyze the condition for glans covering
Covering of the glans (or its measure if amputated) must be complete in the front or back passage of a living human who is capable of intercourse ('حي يجامع مثله'). This excludes animals, corpses, and those not sexually active.
A pubescent girl is considered capable even if not penetrated.
4
Analyze the condition for corpse/animal intercourse
Mere intercourse with a corpse or animal does not require ghusl; actual emission of maniy must occur. The text emphasizes the condition 'إنزال المني' (emission of maniy).
This distinguishes from the second cause where covering alone suffices.
5
Apply to scenarios
Use the conditions to test hypothetical cases: e.g., a wet dream with no desire, insertion without emission, etc. Determine whether ghusl is obligatory in each case.
This step consolidates understanding of the conditions.

The Book of Purification: The meanings that necessitate ghusl: his seminal fluid with desire without intercourse, and covering of the glans and its measure from its amputee in one of the two passages of the head of a living human male's penis, and the emission of maniy by intercourse with a corpse or an animal, and the existence of water... as it is. (Commentary: 'Maniy' means the place of maniy: the spine and the ribs. 'With desire': if you say: why did the Shaykh not say 'with desire and gushing' as is well-known among them? We say: the condition of desire suffices over gushing due to its necessity; al-Baydawi said: 'and gushing water' meaning possessing gushing, which is a pouring in which there is force. 'Without intercourse': it is general, including if maniy came out from memory, looking, play, or wet dream, even if for the first time at puberty in the correct view. It is said: ghusl is not obligatory by wet dream the first time at puberty because... and the restriction by 'for puberty' is to exclude if puberty was confirmed first without emission then he emits, ghusl is obligatory. 'Covering': when the glans is hidden in front or back of a living human if the penis is intact; if the head is amputated and the measure of the glans disappears in one of them, wudu is nullified. This is absolute but restricted to when the entire glans is hidden; if less than it or less than its measure from the amputee disappears, ghusl is not obligatory as in al-Qahistani. The glans, as in al-Qamus: what is above the circumcision; here it means the head of the penis. 'Human' excludes animal penis. 'Desired' excludes a penis that is not desired and the amputated one. 'Living' excludes the penis of a dead person; a pubescent girl requires ghusl by the covering of the adolescent's glans. 'Human': when the glans is hidden in one of the two passages of a living human; by 'human' we exclude other than him like animals and corpses; his saying 'living' is absolute but restricted to one who is sexually active; ghusl is not obligatory by intercourse with these things and wudu is not nullified; rather, washing his penis is obligatory as in al-Qahistani from al-Nawaqid. Included in our saying 'living who can have intercourse' is a young girl who is desired even if not penetrated because she has become one who can have intercourse in the correct view. 'Emission of maniy etc.' The condition of emission; because mere intercourse with them does not necessitate ghusl nor nullify wudu.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Nur al-Idah listing the causes of ghusl (obligatory full-body wash). The first cause is maniy exiting with sexual desire, regardless of whether it gushes or not; the inclusion of desire suffices because gushing normally accompanies it. This includes any stimulation (memory, looking, playing, wet dream) even at the first instance of puberty, though there is a weak opinion that no ghusl is required for the first wet dream at puberty. The second cause is the insertion of the glans or its equivalent into the front or back passage of a living human, even if the penis is amputated (the measure of the glans must be hidden). The third cause is emission of maniy due to intercourse with a corpse or an animal, but only if actual emission occurs; mere insertion without emission does not necessitate ghusl. The commentary clarifies each condition, distinguishing between different types of partners and ensuring that the one being penetrated is alive and capable of intercourse (including a young girl who is marriageable).

Key Terms
حشفة (hashafah): The glans of the penis, defined as the part above the circumcision.
مذي (madhy): Pre-ejaculatory fluid; its exit does not necessitate ghusl but requires washing the area and wudu.
إنزال (inzal): Emission of maniy; actual discharge, which is a condition for the third cause of ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The glans must be covered in any orifice of a living or dead human.
B) The glans must be covered in the front or back passage of a living human who is capable of intercourse.
C) Any part of the penis must be inserted into a living human.
D) The glans must be covered in a living animal.

Key Takeaway
Each cause of ghusl has specific conditions that must be met; desire for maniy, a living capable partner for glans covering, and actual emission for corpse/animal intercourse.
